colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">B ([O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>]3727)</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.8972 \u00b1 0.0004</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">9 \u00b1 2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">4 \u00b1 2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Note.</strong> Lensing features of confirmed systems and properties of associated redMAPPer clusters. In the left section, we show the DESDM object IDs for lenses (as in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t1\">1</a>), the source image IDs, and the emission lines identified in the source spectra. In the middle section, we show the photometric redshifts of lenses <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub>, spectroscopic redshifts <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub>, Einstein radii <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>, and derived enclosed masses <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub>. Ellipsis points indicate features that were not measured. For all of the systems, photometric redshifts (drawn from the the DESDM database) were obtained for the lensing galaxies. The uncertainties have been multiplied by 1.5 times the original estimate, according to the results of S\u00e1nchez et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib69\">2014</a>) for estimating uncertainties in DES photometric redshift measurement codes. The photometric redshifts of the sources within the DESDM database were not found to be reliable when compared with the spectroscopic redshift measurements made in this work: the source redshifts are too large for accurate photometric estimations. Redshifts for the sources are those measured from spectroscopic follow-up at Gemini South and Magellan; please see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s4\">4</a> for details of the spectroscopic follow-up. Only one lensing galaxy has a spectroscopic redshift: in DES J0330\u20135228, we used that measured by Werner et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>). For five of the six systems, morphology indicates a single Einstein radius and enclosed mass for a given system. For each of the systems DES J0221\u20130646 and DES J0446\u20135126, we measured a single Einstein radius that intersects two images with the same source redshift. DES J0250\u20130008 and DES J0330\u20135228 each have only one source image with redshifts. DES J0329\u20132820 has two source redshifts, but only one is chosen for the radius measurement. In DES J2336\u20135352, the lower-redshift source has, anomalously, a larger radius; arc B may be outside the caustic and thus only weakly magnified and not multiply imaged. Please see Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s5-1\">5.1</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s5-6\">5.6</a> for details of the analysis in each system. In the rightmost section, we show the properties of the associated redMAPPer clusters. The masses are measured with the methods of Saro et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib70\">2015</a>). The mass calibration method does not reach to low enough masses and richness to provide a complete probability distribution for the mass of this DES J0221\u20130646; we instead cite an upper limit. The redMAPPer redshifts are measured for the entire cluster with the colors of all the cluster members, which affords a much smaller uncertainty in the redMAPPer redshifts than that of the lensing galaxy for all systems.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t3_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/0004-637X/827/1/51\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t3_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>All of the confirmed systems are associated with redMAPPer groups or clusters. The cluster masses were measured with the procedure of Saro et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib70\">2015</a>), which uses redMAPPer and SPT clusters to calibrate richness and mass. The spherical overdensity mass <em>M</em><sub>\u0394</sub> is that enclosed within a radial boundary demarcated by a density contrast \u0394 with respect to the critical density. We summarize the redshifts, richnesses, and masses of these clusters in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t3\">3</a>. The redMAPPer cluster redshifts are measured with the colors of all the cluster members, which affords a much smaller uncertainty in the cluster redshifts than that of the lensing galaxy for all systems. There are then likely to be some differences between the redshift of the single lensing galaxy and that of the cluster. However, except for the group-scale system, DES J0221\u20130646, the photometric redshift of the lensing galaxy and the redMAPPer-determined redshift agree within uncertainties\u2014as they should, since the lensing galaxy is within the cluster.</p><p>We estimate frequentist uncertainties for the enclosed mass, which include uncertainties propagated from redshifts (via the angular diameter distances) and Einstein radii. The photometric redshift uncertainties have been multiplied by a factor of 1.5, according to the prescription of S\u00e1nchez et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib69\">2014</a>) for photometric redshift measurements in DES. The spectroscopic redshift uncertainties are the result of a sum in quadrature of the wavelength calibration uncertainty and the redshift determination uncertainty from the IRAF function <tt>emsao</tt>.</p><p>The mass uncertainty is the sum in quadrature of the contributions from the Einstein radius and the three angular diameter distances. The uncertainty on each angular diameter distance scales with the redshift error, which varies from ~0.01% to ~5%, while uncertainty in the Einstein radius is at least ~15% among all systems. This disparity sets the Einstein radius as the dominant contributor to the uncertainty. In the manual fit, the uncertainty in the radius arises from variation of the arc's curvature over its extent, with the assumption that the correct center is chosen, as defined in Diehl et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib25\">2009</a>). These uncertainties are estimated to be up to a few arcseconds. The distances between the source and lens cause some variation in the relative contributions to uncertainties: smaller distances provide for a greater contribution from the angular diameter distance <em>D</em><sub>LS</sub>. This results in mass uncertainties in the range ~30%\u201380% (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t3\">3</a>).</p><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-1\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-1\">5.1.\u00a0DES J0221\u20130646</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0221\u20130646 was originally discovered in CFHTLS (Object SA39; More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>) and encountered during our nontargeted search. It was also serendipitously found to be a group-scale lens in the redMAPPer catalog, with richness 4.64 \u00b1 1.15 and redshift 0.603 \u00b1 0.025. The central red galaxy of the lens has a DESDM photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.672 \u00b1 0.042. There are two prominent blue arcs, A1 and A2, shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(a), to the southeast and southwest of the lensing galaxy, respectively. These arcs are also shown in the top and middle panels on the right of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f2\">2</a>. In the follow-up GMOS spectroscopy of both arcs, we identify strong emission lines (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f2\">2</a>, left panel) at the same observed wavelength (4535 \u00c5) in both B600 spectra. Taking into account the absence of other spectral features in the R150 spectra, along with the photometric redshift of the lens galaxy, we assign the features to be Ly<em>\u03b1</em>, which gives redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 2.7251 \u00b1 0.0008 and 2.7241 \u00b1 0.0008 for arcs A1 and A2, respectively.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0DES J0221\u20130646. The 1D spectra for A1 and A2 are shown on the left. There are strong emission lines at the same observed wavelength 4535 \u00c5 in both spectra. Taking into account the absence of other spectral features in the R150 spectra, along with the photometric redshift of the lens galaxy, we assign the features to be Ly<em>\u03b1</em>, which gives redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 2.7251 \u00b1 0.0008 and 2.7241 \u00b1 0.0008 for A1 and A2, respectively. The orientation of the slits (red boxes) is shown in the upper right DES <em>g</em>-band image. In the color coadd image in the middle right panel, the two features of interest are labeled \"A1\" and \"A2,\" the Einstein radius is marked by a white arc, and the small scale bar shows the size of the image. In the lower right is an identical color coadd image. All images are oriented north up, east left. The flat, zero-count portions of the spectra near <em>\u03bb</em> ~ 4200 and ~5300 \u00c5 show the chip gaps in the detectors: we did not take an offset observation to cover the chip gaps for this system.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We estimate an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 6'' \u00b1 1'', which passes through both arcs, giving an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (1 \u00b1 0.4) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</p><p>In the DES imaging, there appear two arcs that may be counterimages. An arc north of arc A2, and extremely close to the lensing galaxy, may be a counterimage to arc A1. These objects appear more clearly in Figure 3, panel \"39\" of More et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>). If we assume that arc A1 has a counterpart to the northeast, we can perform a manual measurement of the image-splitting distance: for lensing systems where the source lies nearly along the line of sight through the lens, the distance between an image and its counterimage is related to the Einstein radius, \u0394<em>\u03b8</em> ~ 2<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>. For this system, \u0394<em>\u03b8</em> = 8'' \u00b1 2'', which gives <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> ~ 4'' \u00b1 2'', in agreement with the direct manual measurement of the Einstein radius.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0DES J0250\u20130008. The 1D spectrum for A is shown on the left. There is a strong emission line at an observed wavelength of 8229 \u00c5. We associate this feature with [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, which yields a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.2081 \u00b1 0.0004. The orientation of the slits (red boxes) is shown in the upper right DES <em>r</em>-band image. In the color coadd image in the middle right panel, the two features of interest are labeled \"A\" and \"B,\" the Einstein radius is marked by a white arc, and small scale bar shows the size of the image. In the lower right is an identical color coadd image. All images are oriented north up, east left.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f3\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>There is yet another possible counterimage slightly north and east of arc A1. These counterimages were not targeted: for a single multi-slit mask, it was not possible to obtain spectra for these objects simultaneously with that of the brighter arcs we had chosen (see top right panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f2\">2</a> for the configuration of the mask slits).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-2\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-2\">5.2.\u00a0DES J0250\u20130008</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0250\u20130008 is a newly discovered system identified in the redMAPPer cluster sample during the targeted search. The central red lensing galaxy has a photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.841 \u00b1 0.042, with two blue features labeled A and B in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(b) and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f3\">3</a> (top and middle right panels); the arcs are located east and north of the central cluster galaxy, respectively. We identify a strong emission line for A (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f3\">3</a>, left panel) in the GMOS R150 spectrum at an observed wavelength of 8229 \u00c5. We associate this feature with [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, which yields a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.2081 \u00b1 0.0004. We do not detect any continuum, nor any emission or absorption lines, from arc B.</p><p>While we do not detect features in the arc B spectrum, its morphology suggests that it is likely to be a lensed source image. Assuming that it is a feature of this system, we located the lensing mass center such that it aligns with the tangential arc B. As the circle of the Einstein radius passes through both images A and B, we use the spectroscopic redshift of arc A, and we estimate a single Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 7'' \u00b1 2'' for this system, which gives an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (4 \u00b1 2)  <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. The source of image A may lie outside the caustic and thus may not be strongly lensed, only weakly magnified. In such a case, this would provide a poor estimate of the enclosed mass.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-3\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-3\">5.3.\u00a0DES J0329\u20132820</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0329\u20132820 is also a newly discovered system identified in the redMAPPer galaxy cluster sample during the targeted search. The central red galaxy has a photometric redshift <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.655 \u00b1 0.033. There are two small blue arcs, A and B, to the west and north of the central red galaxy in the image, shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(c) and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f4\">4</a>, top and middle right panels. In the follow-up IMACS spectroscopy of the arcs, we identify emission lines at two different observed wavelengths of 6694 and 8563 \u00c5. The GMOS B600 data for this system provide no observed features at shorter wavelengths; therefore, we identify the emission line as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, from which we obtain redshifts <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.7963 \u00b1 0.0001 and 1.2976 \u00b1 0.0003 for images A and B, respectively (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f4\">4</a>, left panel).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0DES J0329\u20132820. The 1D spectra for A and B are shown on the left. There are emission lines at two different observed wavelengths, 6694 \u00c5 and 8563 \u00c5. Given the lack of observed features at shorter wavelengths, we identify the emission as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, from which we obtain redshifts <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.7963 \u00b1 0.0001 and 1.2976 \u00b1 0.0003 for A and B, respectively. We therefore elect to use only A (the circle for which also goes through B). We then estimate a single Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 7'' \u00b1 2'' for A, which gives an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (6 \u00b1 4) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-4\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-4\">5.4.\u00a0DES J0330\u20135228</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0330\u20135228 was discovered with <em>XMM-Newton</em>\u2014via detection of a second X-ray peak (Werner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>)\u2014as a cluster behind Abell 3128. It has also been measured in both the SPT (SPT-CL J0330\u20135228; Ruel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib67\">2014</a>) and ACT SZ surveys (ACT-CL J0330\u20135227; Menanteau et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib54\">2010b</a>). The central red galaxy has a photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.463 \u00b1 0.046. There is a very prominent blue arc (A) in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(d) to the southwest of the central lensing galaxy. In the follow-up GMOS R150 spectroscopy of the blue arc, we identify a strong emission line at an observed wavelength of 9146 \u00c5, which we take to be [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, from which we obtain a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.4541 \u00b1 0.0004 (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f5\">5</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0DES J0330\u20135228. The 1D spectrum for A is shown on the left. There is a strong emission line at an observed wavelength of 9146 \u00c5, which we take to be [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, from which we obtain a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.4541 \u00b1 0.0004. We estimate an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 8'' \u00b1 2'' for this system. Instead of the DES photometric redshift, we used the spectroscopic redshift from Werner et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>), <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.43961 \u00b1 0.00014, to estimate an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (2 \u00b1 0.8) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. Werner et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>) also estimate an Einstein radius of ~6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 and an upper limit on the mass of ~5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>12</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>; the authors noted that they only had a redshift measurement for the lens. Without a spectroscopic redshift for the source, the authors assumed a combined angular diameter distance of <em>D</em><sub>L</sub> <em>D</em><sub>S</sub>/<em>D</em><sub>LS</sub> = 1 Gpc, which corresponds to an overestimate of the source redshift and thus an underestimate of the mass (see Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2eqn1\">1</a>)).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-5\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-5\">5.5.\u00a0DES J0446\u20135126</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0446\u20135126 is also a newly discovered system, identified in the redMAPPer cluster-finder sample. Two small blue arcs, B1 and B2, lie to the northwest and southwest, respectively, of the central red galaxy; this is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(e) and in the top and middle right panels of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f6\">6</a>. The candidate lensing galaxy has a photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.746 \u00b1 0.047, in agreement with the redMAPPer cluster photometric redshift, <em>z</em><sub>RM</sub> = 0.734 \u00b1 0.020.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0DES J0446\u20135126. The 1D spectra for B1 and B2 are shown on the left. The spectrum for A is not shown, but has an emission line consistent with an interpretation as a foreground object. There are prominent emission lines in both arcs at the same observed wavelength of 5117 \u00c5. We conclude that this is Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission, from which we obtain redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 3.2068 \u00b1 0.0010 for B and 3.2086 \u00b1 0.0011 for C. The object labeled A also has emission lines (not shown), which we interpret as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727 and [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>5007. This gives a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.1680 \u00b1 0.0009, making object A a foreground object. The orientation of the slits (red boxes) is shown in the upper right DES <em>g</em>-band image. In the color coadd image in the middle right panel the three features of interest are labeled \"A,\" \"B1,\" and \"B2,\" the Einstein radius is marked by a white arc, and the small scale bar shows the size of the image. In the lower right is an identical color coadd image. All images are oriented north up, east left.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The GMOS B600 spectroscopy reveals prominent emission lines (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f6\">6</a>, left panel) in both arcs at the same observed wavelength of 5117 \u00c5. Given the appreciable strength of these emission lines in the B600 spectra, we expected to see other strong lines, such as H<em>\u03b2</em> in the R150 spectrum. However, additional features did not appear, and we therefore concluded that this emission line is Ly<em>\u03b1</em>, from which we obtain spectroscopic redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 3.2068 \u00b1 0.0010 and 3.2086 \u00b1 0.0011 for B1 and B2, respectively. The object labeled A in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(e) also has emission lines (not shown), which we interpret as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727 and [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>5007. This gives a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.1680 \u00b1 0.0009, making object A a foreground object.</p><p>We estimate an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 7'' \u00b1 2'' for this system, which gives an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (2 \u00b1 0.9) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-6\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-6\">5.6.\u00a0DES J2336\u20135352</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J2336\u20135352 is a galaxy cluster originally found in the SCS, and it has a cluster photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>c</sub> = 0.54 (SCSO J233607\u2013535235; Menanteau et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib53\">2010a</a>). It is also found in the redMAPPer cluster-finder sample, which gives a photometric redshift <em>z</em><sub>RM</sub> = 0.522 \u00b1 0.011, in agreement with the DES photometric redshift, <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.530 \u00b1 0.075.</p><p>There are two small red arcs, A and B, to the west and southeast of the central red galaxy, respectively. These are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(f) and in the top and middle right panels of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f7\">7</a>. The GMOS R150 spectra of these two arcs show prominent emission lines at two different observed wavelengths\u20148023 \u00c5 for A and 7070 \u00c5 for B\u2014which we identify as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727. These yield source redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.1528 \u00b1 0.0006 and 0.8972 \u00b1 0.0004 for A and B, respectively (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f7\">7</a>, left panel).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0DES J2336\u20135352. The 1D spectra for A and B are shown on the left. The prominent emission lines at two different observed wavelengths of 8023 \u00c5 for A and 7070 \u00c5 for B are due to [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727. These lines yield source spectroscopic redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.1528 \u00b1 0.0006 and 0.8972 \u00b1 0.0004 for A and B, respectively. The orientation of the slits (red boxes) is shown in the upper right DES <em>g</em>-band image. In the color coadd image in the middle right panel the two features of interest are labeled \"A\" and \"B.\" The Einstein radii are marked by white and red arcs for slits A and B, respectively. The small scale bar shows the size of the image. In the lower right is an identical color coadd image. All images are oriented north up, east left.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We estimate an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 5'' \u00b1 2'' for A and <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 9'' \u00b1 2'' for B, which give enclosed masses of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (9 \u00b1 7) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>12</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> and <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (4 \u00b1 2) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, respectively.</p><p>We note that normally one would expect the lower-redshift source to have a smaller <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> than the higher-redshift one, but that does not seem to be the case here. Arc B may be outside the caustic, and thus only weakly magnified and not multiply imaged; it is also not clearly tangentially aligned with the candidate lens, which makes it less likely to be a lensed source.</p><p>There also exists a faint image to the west of arc B, almost directly south of the central red lensing galaxy, that may be another source or counterimage. To provide sufficient sky coverage in the slit for arc B, this image could not also be observed in a single mask.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s6\" name=\"apjaa23f2s6\">6.\u00a0DISCUSSION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Many of our unconfirmed systems exhibit clear lensing features but have no detectable continuum or spectral features, with integration times of about 1 hr on an 8 m class telescope.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn16\">62</a></sup>\n The need for long integration time highlights the challenge of obtaining spectra for faint objects found by deep optical surveys, like DES: the challenge will only be greater for the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn17\">63</a></sup>\n (LSST; LSST Science Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib48\">2009</a>). One faces the prospect of long integration times, which limits the number of systems that can be followed up with a typical program. For systems that exhibit a clear lensing morphology and that have detectable continuum emission but no visible features, IR data may be necessary to confirm them.</p><p>A number of large telescopes housing UV to infrared spectroscopic instruments are planned for the coming 10\u201320 yr, which will help to alleviate this pressure. The Thirty Meter Telescope<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn18\">64</a></sup>\n will require a mere hour of integration on <em>i</em> ~ 25 for low-noise spectroscopic measurements\u2014similarly for the Giant Magellan Telescope.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn19\">65</a></sup>\n However, with their expected high subscription rate, it remains to be seen whether there will be enough time to follow up the systems predicted for projects like LSST.</p><p>While Gemini's GMOS instrument covers optical wavelengths in multi-slit or long-slit mode, the Very Large Telescope's X-shooter<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn20\">66</a></sup>\n provides long-slit capability for strong-lens follow-up simultaneously in optical and near-IR wavelengths. X-shooter is the first second-generation instrument on the 8 m VLT: it has an echelle spectrograph that (1) covers a spectral range 300\u20132500 nm at intermediate resolution (<em>R</em> ~ 4,000\u201317,000) and (2) operates in long-slit or IFU mode (Vernet et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib77\">2011</a>). In addition, new instruments are being proposed for the Gemini Observatories via the Gemini Instrument Feasibility study.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn21\">67</a></sup>\n For example, the Gemini Multi-Obect eXtra-wide-band Spectrograph (GMOX; Robberto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib66\">2015</a>) has a three-arm spectrograph covering the entire optical and near-IR spectrum that is available from the ground. Another example, OCTOCAM, is a multi-channel imager and long-slit spectrograph, also covering the optical and near-IR wavelengths (de Ugarte Postigo <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib22\">2015</a>). Both GMOX and OCTOCAM add integral field capability, which are ideal for extended lenses. If integration times are sufficiently low for these new instruments, the Gemini observatories may provide a competitive resource for follow-up of lensing systems in future surveys.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s7\" name=\"apjaa23f2s7\">7.\u00a0SUMMARY</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this paper we presented the first confirmed group- and cluster-scale strong-lensing systems found in DES data. These systems were discovered through visual identification using both targeted and nontargeted searches. We confirmed these systems with a combination of spectroscopy from GMOS on the Gemini South telescope and IMACS on the Magellan/Baade telescope.</p><p>Two of these systems are of particular interest because they have source galaxies at redshifts <em>z</em> = 2.7251 and <em>z</em> = 3.22086. Due to the magnification provided by lensing, these sources are typically among the brightest galaxies in their redshift ranges and can provide an important window into the star formation history and galaxy formation at these cosmic epochs.</p><p>The candidate lensing systems discovered in DES SV data span a range of arc sizes, much like the currently known sample of candidates from searches during the past decade. Due to the seeing limitations of DES (median expected seeing ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9), systems with smaller arc separations were more difficult to identify, especially during the nontargeted search. Thus, our visual inspection methods likely yielded higher completeness in the targeted search of redMAPPer and SPT clusters.</p><p>As we examine more of the DES wide-field area, we expect to find many more systems. Through comparison with the CFHTLS data, we expect there to be ~2100 lenses in the full survey, which is similar to the number found in the preceding decade of survey searches in optical and IR wavelengths listed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s1\">1</a>. Independently, Collett (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib19\">2015</a>) predicts 800 galaxy-scale lenses.</p><p>The Year 1 DES data cover an area of ~1800 deg<sup>2</sup>, and we have already conducted targeted searches, identifying a number of promising systems for follow-up. We have also expanded the search techniques to use arc-finding (e.g., More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>) and ring-finding algorithms (e.g., Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib33\">2014</a>), as well as catalog-based photometry searches similar to those employed in the SBAS (e.g., Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib25\">2009</a>). We continue the dedicated program, STRIDES (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib76\">2015</a>), to search for lensed quasars. With these tools, our searches through Year 1 data will be more effective than in SV for finding galaxy-scale lenses. The high yields of DES and future surveys will likely pose a challenge for spectroscopic follow-up and thus a challenge to maximize strong lensing for use in constraining cosmology.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We are grateful for the extraordinary contributions of our CTIO colleagues and the DES Camera, Commissioning, and Science Verification teams for achieving excellent instrument and telescope conditions that have made this work possible. The success of this project also relies critically on the expertise and dedication of the DES Data Management organization.</p><p>Funding for the DES Projects has been provided by the U.S. Department of Energy, the U.S. National Science Foundation, the Ministry of Science and Education of Spain, the Science and Technology Facilities Council of the United Kingdom, the Higher Education Funding Council for England, the National Center for Supercomputing Applications at the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, the Kavli Institute of Cosmological Physics at the University of Chicago, the Center for Cosmology and Astro-Particle Physics at the Ohio State University, the Mitchell Institute for Fundamental Physics and Astronomy at Texas A&amp;M University, Financiadora de Estudos e Projetos, Funda\u00e7\u00e3o Carlos Chagas Filho de Amparo \u00e0 Pesquisa do Estado do Rio de Janeiro, Conselho Nacional de Desenvolvimento Cientfico e Tecnol\u00f3gico and the Minist\u00e9rio da Ci\u00eancia e Tecnologia, the Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft, and the Collaborating Institutions in the Dark Energy Survey. The DES data management system is supported by the National Science Foundation under Grant Number AST-1138766. The DES participants from Spanish institutions are partially supported by MINECO under grants AYA2012-39559, ESP2013-48274, FPA2013-47986, and Centro de Excelencia Severo Ochoa SEV-2012-0234, some of which include ERDF funds from the European Union.</p><p>The Collaborating Institutions are Argonne National Laboratory, the University of California at Santa Cruz, the University of Cambridge, Centro de Investigaciones Energ\u00e9ticas, Medioambientales y Tecnol\u00f3gicas-Madrid, the University of Chicago, University College London, the DES-Brazil Consortium, the Eidgenoessische Technische Hochschule (ETH) Zurich, Fermi National Accelerator Laboratory, the University of Edinburgh, the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, the Institut de Ci\u00e8ncies de l'Espai (IEEC/CSIC), the Institut de F\u00edsica d'Altes Energies, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, the Ludwig-Maximilians Universit\u00e4t and the associated Excellence Cluster Universe, the University of Michigan, the National Optical Astronomy Observatory, the University of Nottingham, the Ohio State University, the University of Pennsylvania, the University of Portsmouth, SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory, Stanford University, the University of Sussex, and Texas A&amp;M University.</p><p>We are also grateful to Janani Sivakumar, student at the Illinois Mathematics and Science Academy, as well as Caroline Odden and her students, Kathryn Chapman, George Avecillas, and Matthew Simon, at the Phillips Academy, for their contributions to the scanning effort. We also thank Matthew Becker and Risa Wechsler for helpful discussions on the topic of galaxy cluster richnesses and masses.</p><p>This work is based in part on observations obtained at the Gemini Observatory, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc., under a cooperative agreement with the NSF on behalf of the Gemini partnership: the National Science Foundation (United States), the National Research Council (Canada), CONICYT (Chile), the Australian Research Council (Australia), Minist\u00e9rio da Ci\u00eancia, Tecnologia e Inova\u00e7\u00e3o (Brazil), and Ministerio de Ciencia, Tecnolog\u00eda e Innovaci\u00f3n Productiva (Argentina). The data were acquired through the Gemini Science Archive and processed using the Gemini IRAF package v2.16.</p><p>Thanks to the entire Las Campanas Observatory staff for their help in the acquisition of the Magellan/IMACS data (IMACS; Bigelow &amp; Dressler <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib8\">2003</a>). This paper includes data gathered with the 6.5 m Magellan Telescopes located at Las Campanas Observatory, Chile.</p><p>C.F. acknowledges funding from CAPES (proc. 12203-1). This paper has gone through internal review by the DES collaboration. This research has made use of NASA's Astrophysics Data System.</p><p>C.D.B. would like to thank CNPq for the financial support.</p><p>R.J.F. gratefully acknowledges support from the Alfred P. Sloan Foundation.</p><p>Fermilab is operated by Fermi Research Alliance, LLC, under Contract No. DE-AC02-07CH11359 with the United States Department of Energy.</p></div></div><!-- End article full <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">OBSERVATION AND CONFIRMATION OF SIX STRONG-LENSING SYSTEMS IN THE DARK ENERGY SURVEY SCIENCE VERIFICATION DATA<SUP>*</SUP>  </h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">B. -->\n<!-- End Accepted manuscript Link -->\n<!-- Start Annotations -->\n <!-- End Annotations --><div class=\"col-no-break wd-jnl-aas-keywords\">\n\t\t<div class=\"replica-h4\">Keywords</div>\n\t\t<p><a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=11%2F48&amp;code_desc=cosmology%3A+observations\">cosmology: observations</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=11%2F6&amp;code_desc=galaxies%3A+clusters%3A+general\">galaxies: clusters: general</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=11%2F10&amp;code_desc=galaxies%3A+distances+and+redshifts\">galaxies: distances and redshifts</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=2%2F46&amp;code_desc=gravitational+lensing%3A+strong\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>; <a href=\"/searchaaskeyword?source=aas&amp;type=kwd_group&amp;code=3%2F33&amp;code_desc=methods%3A+observational\">methods: observational</a>; <a <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F827%2F1%2F51\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/827/1/51</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>We report the observation and confirmation of the first group- and cluster-scale strong gravitational lensing systems found in Dark Energy Survey data. Through visual inspection of data from the Science Verification season, we identified 53 candidate systems. We then obtained spectroscopic follow-up of 21 candidates using the Gemini Multi-object Spectrograph at the Gemini South telescope and the Inamori-Magellan Areal Camera and Spectrograph at the Magellan/Baade telescope. With this follow-up, we confirmed six candidates as gravitational lenses: three of the systems are newly discovered, and the remaining three were previously known. Of the 21 observed candidates, the remaining 15 either were not detected in spectroscopic observations, were observed and did not exhibit continuum emission (or spectral features), or were ruled out as lensing systems. The confirmed sample consists of one group-scale and five galaxy-cluster-scale lenses. The lensed sources range in redshift <I>z</I> ~ 0.80\u20133.2 and in <I>i</I>-band surface brightness <I>i</I> <SUB>SB</SUB> ~ 23\u201325 mag arcsec<SUP>\u22122</SUP> (2'' aperture). For each of the six systems, we estimate the Einstein radius <I>\u03b8</I> <SUB>E</SUB> and the enclosed mass <I>M</I> <SUB>enc</SUB>, which have ranges <I>\u03b8</I> <SUB>E</SUB> ~ 5''\u20139'' and <I>M</I> <SUB>enc</SUB> ~ 8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<SUP>12</SUP> to 6 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<SUP>13</SUP> <I>M</I> <SUB>\u2299</SUB>, respectively.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/827/1/51&doi=10.3847/0004-637X/827/1/51&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/827/1/51&doi=10.3847/0004-637X/827/1/51&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2016ApJ...827...51N\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2016ApJ...827...51N&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2016ApJ...827...51N\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s1\" name=\"apjaa23f2s1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Strong gravitational lensing of galaxies and quasars provides opportunities to study cosmology, dark matter, dark energy, galactic structure, and galaxy evolution (Treu <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib75\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib75\">2010</a>). Strong lensing also provides a sample of galaxies\u2014the lenses themselves\u2014that are selected based on total mass, rather than luminosity or surface brightness (e.g., Reblinsky &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib64\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib64\">1999</a>): the distortion of images by the lens depends only on the total lens mass and its spatial distribution.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn2\">52</a></sup>\n\n</p><p>The majority of strong-lensing systems discovered in the past decade were found with a variety of techniques, mostly through dedicated investigations of existing data or through new dedicated surveys. Data from the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib83\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib83\">2000</a>), for example, have been used by a number of groups to select lens candidates. The Optimal Line-of-Sight-lens Survey (Willis et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib82\">2006</a>) discovered five galaxy-scale lenses. Investigating a sample of rich galaxy clusters, Hennawi et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib35\">2008</a>) found 37 lenses of varying size and morphology. The Sloan Bright Arc Survey (SBAS; Allam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib2\">2007</a>; Kubik <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib42\">2007</a>; Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib25\">2009</a>; Kubo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib43\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib43\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib44\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib44\">2010</a>; Lin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib47\">2009</a>; Wiesner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib81\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib81\">2012</a>) discovered 28 galaxy- and group-scale systems. The Sloan Lens ACS Survey group (Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib13\">2008</a>) identified 137 lensing candidates, using both the spectroscopic and imaging catalogs. The CAmbridge Sloan Survey Of Wide ARcs in the skY (Belokurov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib4\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib4\">2009</a>) searched for wide-separation, galaxy-scale systems, uncovering 45 systems to date. Other searches, like the Strong Lensing Legacy Survey (SL2S; e.g., Cabanac et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib17\">2007</a>; More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>), have yielded 127 lens candidates\u2014spanning group to cluster scales\u2014from ~150 deg<sup>2</sup> of sky area in the Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope Legacy Survey (CFHTLS). Searches in the COSMOS field (Faure et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib29\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib29\">2008</a>; Jackson <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib38\">2008</a>) have yielded 70 lens candidates.</p><p>Surveys in other bands have also proven successful. The Cosmic Lens All-Sky Survey<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn3\">53</a></sup>\n (CLASS; e.g., Browne et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib14\">2003</a>; Myers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib58\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib58\">2003</a>; York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib84\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib84\">2005</a>) confirmed nearly 25 radio-loud galaxy-scale lensing systems. Several of them have multiply imaged sources, including the four-image system CLASS B2045+265 (Fassnacht et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib28\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib28\">1999</a>) and the two-component wide-separation (4<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />56) system CLASS B2108+213 (McKean et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib51\">2005</a>).</p><p>There is a history of targeting massive galaxy clusters to use as cosmic lenses for the study of high-redshift star-forming galaxies in the infrared (e.g., Smail et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib72\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib72\">1997</a>; Egami et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib27\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib27\">2010</a>; Swinbank et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib74\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib74\">2010</a>). Recently, wide-field millimeter and submillimeter surveys have enabled the discovery of new populations of strong-lensing systems based on a simple flux-density selection (Blain <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib10\">1996</a>; Negrello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib60\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib60\">2007</a>) with near-100% completeness (Negrello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib61\">2010</a>; Vieira et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib78\">2010</a>; Bussmann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib16\">2013</a>). These wide-field surveys, coupled with the recently commissioned Atacama Large Millimeter Array (ALMA), have opened a new window to star formation at high redshifts (e.g., Vieira et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib79\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib79\">2013</a>; ALMA Partnership et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib3\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib3\">2015</a>).</p><p>These investigations\u2014from optical to millimeter wavebands\u2014produced on the order of 1000 lensing candidates or confirmed lenses, spanning a range of arc sizes\u2014from small arcs (<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> ~ 3'') produced by single-galaxy lenses to giant arcs typically found in clusters (<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> ~ 10'').</p><p>The Dark Energy Survey (DES<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn4\">54</a></sup>\n; Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib24\">2014</a>; Flaugher et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib30\">2015</a>)\u2014a new, deep sky survey covering 5000 deg<sup>2</sup> of the Southern Galactic Cap in five optical filters (<em>grizY</em>)\u2014is suitable for a systematic census of gravitational lenses. The wide-field survey will have a depth of <em>i</em> ~ 24 mag at 10<em>\u03c3</em> detection threshold, and the 30 deg<sup>2</sup>  supernova survey is spread over 10 fields\u2014eight shallow and two deep. The primary instrument for DES is the newly assembled Dark Energy Camera (DECam), a wide-field (3 deg<sup>2</sup>) CCD mosaic camera located on the Blanco 4 m telescope at the Cerro Tololo Inter-American Observatory in the Chilean Andes. The DES footprint is best observed between August and mid-February. DES observes for ~105 nights yr<sup>\u22121</sup> and has completed 3 of the 5 yr of planned observations. The survey will produce the widest, deepest, and most uniform contiguous map in optical wavelengths of the southern sky, much of which has not been systematically surveyed\u2014and not at such depth\u2014until now. These features make DES suitable for finding many strong-lensing systems that span large ranges in redshift and Einstein radii. DES expects to uncover a large and diverse sample of strong gravitational lenses, which will provide for a rich science program.</p><p>One of the main objectives of the strong-lensing science program in DES is to derive constraints on dark energy. One of the two key components will be exploiting galaxy- and cluster-scale lenses that have multiple background sources at different redshifts. When there exist two lensed sources, one source will lie between the lens and the other, more distant source: these sources produce distinct Einstein radii. The ratio of the Einstein radii (and thus, a ratio of angular diameter distances) is independent of the Hubble constant and only weakly dependent on the lens mass distribution. Provided that the lensing mass can be well modeled, the ratio of distance ratios can help to constrain dark energy models (Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib34\">2008</a>; Jullo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib40\">2010</a>; Collett et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib21\">2012</a>; Collett &amp; Auger <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib20\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib20\">2014</a>).</p><p>The second key component is to use strongly lensed quasars to measure cosmological parameters (e.g., <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>) by utilizing the time delays between multiple images (Refsdal <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib65\">1964</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib73\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib73\">2013</a>; Birrer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib9\">2015</a>) of time-varying sources. We predict that the wide-field survey should contain about 120 lensed quasars brighter than <em>i</em> = 21 mag (Oguri &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib62\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib62\">2010</a>), of which ~20 would be high-information-content quadruple-image configurations. Through the STRong-lensing Insights into DES (STRIDES) program,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn5\">55</a></sup>\n DES has discovered and confirmed two lensed quasars so far at <em>z</em> ~ 1.6 and ~2.4 (Agnello et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib1\">2015</a>). In addition, 30 deg<sup>2</sup> of deep imaging in the supernova fields, which have a high cadence, provides an opportunity to find lensed supernovae.</p><p>In addition to constraining cosmology, we will use a substantial cluster-scale lens sample to study dark matter mass profiles. Also, we expect to discover a large sample of sources at varying redshifts that will be valuable for studies of galaxy evolution.</p><p>In this paper, we report on the search for strong lens systems from the DES Science Verification (SV) data. We performed a visual inspection of the ~250 deg<sup>2</sup>  SV area, discovering a large number of candidates, which we then culled to 53 high-ranking candidates. We selected 24 for spectroscopic follow-up and were able to observe 21 of them. Of the sample we followed up, we focus on six systems that have compelling evidence (color, morphology, and spectroscopy) of strong gravitational lensing: three of these are newly identified, and three are already known from other surveys. We have spectroscopically confirmed these six systems (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>) as strong gravitational lensing systems.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Color coadded DES images of the six systems described in this work: (a) DES J0221\u20130646, (b) DES J0250\u20130008, (c) DES J0329\u20132820, (d) DES J0330\u20135228, (e) DES J0446\u20135126, (f) DES J2336\u20135352. All images are oriented north up, east left, and are 1' <b>&#x00d7;</b> 1' in area. The lensing features targeted for spectroscopy are indicated by the letters. Refer to Figures <a href=\"#apjaa23f2f2\">2</a>\u2013<a href=\"#apjaa23f2f7\">7</a> for a more detailed view of the field and the image candidates.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Several of the candidate systems were selected for the possibility of housing sources at multiple redshifts, key for our dark energy studies. While we do not endeavor in this work to perform detailed mass modeling of cluster-scale lenses with DES data, future higher-resolution imaging follow-up may allow for detailed modeling of cluster mass distributions.</p><p>The paper is organized as follows. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s2\">2</a> we describe the DES SV season data. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s3\">3</a> we present the lens search, followed by the spectroscopic follow-up in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s4\">4</a>. We present the six confirmed systems and their properties in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s5\">5</a>, discuss results in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s6\">6</a>, and conclude in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s7\">7</a>. All magnitudes are in the AB system. We assume a flat, Planck \u039bCDM cosmology: \u03a9<sub>M</sub> = 0.308, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.692, and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> = 67.8 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> (Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib63\">2015</a>).</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s2\" name=\"apjaa23f2s2\">2.\u00a0DES SV DATA</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The SV observing season took place from 2012 November 1 to 2013 February 23, immediately after the commissioning of the DECam (Flaugher et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib30\">2015</a>). The survey started in 2013 August and has completed three seasons beyond SV. The survey and operations for SV and Year 1 are described in Diehl et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib24\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib24\">2014</a>).</p><p>In total, SV observations cover ~250 deg<sup>2</sup> in five optical filter bands (<em>grizY</em>), nearly reaching the expected full 5 yr depth of DES: below, we report median depth estimates of these data at 10<em>\u03c3</em> galaxy limiting magnitude. The wide-field survey consists of the SPT-E and SPT-W fields that are located, respectively, in the east and west regions of the South Pole Telescope (SPT) survey footprint (Carlstrom et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib18\">2011</a>): the <em>g</em>-band depth extends to ~24.5 mag. The 30 deg<sup>2</sup> supernova survey takes place in eight shallow fields and two deep fields, extending to <em>g</em>-band depths, 25.0 and 25.4 mag, respectively.</p><p>The DES data management (DESDM) team was responsible for the reduction of the SV images. The basic reductions and coadd source detection were performed by DESDM (Desai et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib23\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib23\">2012</a>; R. Gruendl 2015, in preparation): the software for reduction of SV exposures is similar to that used in the Y1A1 release.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn6\">56</a></sup>\n The detrended, single-epoch images were calibrated, background-subtracted, and combined into \"coadd tiles.\" Each coadd tile has dimensions 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />72 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/fdg.gif\" alt=\"fdg\" align=\"baseline\" />72, so defined as to cover the entire DES footprint. A catalog of objects was extracted from the coadded images using Source Extractor (SExtractor; Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib7\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib7\">1996</a>; Bertin <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib6\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib6\">2011</a>). Unless otherwise stated, photometric and position data of all objects in this work originate in this object catalog (see Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t1\">1</a>).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa23f2t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nProperties of Lensing Systems: Positions and Photometry of Lenses and Source Images\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2t1\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t1_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 1\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">System ID and</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Object ID</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">R.A.
