All positions (R.A., decl. in J2000) and magnitudes are drawn from the DESDM database, except in the case of source image B2 of DES J0446\u20135126: for this object, we measured aperture magnitudes via the Graphical Astronomy and Image Analysis software tool (GAIA <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib32\">2015</a>). <em>Y</em>-band photometry is provided where available: supernova fields were only observed in <em>Y</em>band for those fields that overlap with the wide-field survey, and arc B of DES J0329\u20132820 was too faint for detection in both DESDM and GAIA. Alternative identifications related to prior observations of some systems are listed as footnotes to this table.</p>\n<a name=\"apjaa23f2fn7\" id=\"apjaa23f2fn7\"></a><sup> a</sup>Object SA39: from More et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>).\n<a name=\"apjaa23f2fn8\" id=\"apjaa23f2fn8\"></a><sup> b</sup>SPT-CL J0330\u20135228/ACT-CL J0330\u20135227: from Bleem et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib11\">2015</a>).\n<a name=\"apjaa23f2fn9\" id=\"apjaa23f2fn9\"></a><sup> c</sup>Associated with radio source SUMSS J033057\u2013522811 (Mauch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib50\">2003</a>; Werner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>).\n<a name=\"apjaa23f2fn10\" id=\"apjaa23f2fn10\"></a><sup> d</sup>SCSO J233607\u2013535235: from Menanteau et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib53\">2010a</a>).\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/0004-637X/827/1/51\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s3\" name=\"apjaa23f2s3\">3.\u00a0LENS SEARCH</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The lens search, carried out by visually inspecting images, relied on morphology and color to identify lens candidates. We searched for arc- and ring-like features and multiply imaged sources, in association with red lensing galaxies or galaxy groups. Among the more typical of search criteria is the existence of blue source images in association with red galaxies, but some systems with red source images were also identified.</p><p>Although modern algorithms for automated arc- and ring-finders have improved in recent years, they still typically require manual intervention and/or subsequent visual inspection and verification (Lenzen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib46\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib46\">2004</a>; Horesh et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib37\">2005</a>; Seidel &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib71\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib71\">2007</a>; Marshall et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib49\">2009</a>; More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>; Joseph et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib39\">2014</a>). We performed a visual search without automated finders, because the SV sky area is relatively small and visual inspection is likely to recover the highest-ranking candidates that would be found with an automated finder. Given these search criteria, as well as the pixel scale of DECam and the seeing of DES data, there is a bias toward larger Einstein radii, and thus higher mass (depending on the source redshift(s)), which are easier to identify through visual inspection.</p><p>We performed two types of visual scans. We performed a nontargeted search of all ~250 deg<sup>2</sup>\u2014not focusing on any specific target regions or objects\u2014as well as a targeted scan of previously identified galaxy clusters. The visual scan is performed on false-color PNG images, which are made by combining <em>g</em>, <em>r</em>, <em>i</em> coadd tiles (defined in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s2\">2</a>) into color images.</p><p>The nontargeted search was carried out by about 20 inspectors, who systematically examined all the tiles. The open-area search yielded a sample of ~1000 objects. Among the results of this search is the Cosmic Horseshoe (Belokurov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib5\">2007</a>), which was then removed from our sample of follow-up candidates.</p><p>We also carried out two targeted searches. The first one was carried out around the 67 known clusters from the SPT SZ survey (Bleem et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib11\">2015</a>). Many of these clusters already have optical imaging follow-up and known lensing features. We identified nine candidates from this search. The second targeted search examined 374 galaxy clusters of richness &gt;50, which themselves were found using the red-sequence Matched-filter Probabilistic Percolation cluster-finder algorithm (redMAPPer; Rykoff et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib68\">2014</a>). This search yielded 39 candidates. The targeted search recovered known, confirmed lensing systems, such as BCS J2352\u20135452 (Buckley-Geer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib15\">2011</a>), RXC J2248.7\u20134431 (B\u00f6hringer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib12\">2004</a>), and ACT-CL J0102\u20134915 (El Gordo; Menanteau et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib55\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib55\">2012</a>), which we then removed from the sample of candidates for spectroscopic follow-up.</p><p>The combined nontargeted and targeted search samples were merged and assessed by a team of experienced inspectors. Most of these objects were not convincing as strong lens system candidates or were not bright enough to expect successful spectroscopic follow-up. Each of the systems was assigned a rank, 1 to 3\u2014with 1 signifying least likely to be a lens system, and 3 the most likely\u2014on the basis of morphology, color, and brightness of sources. This ranking resulted in 53 promising (\"rank 3\") candidates.</p><p>To characterize the selection function of our sample, we compare to a sample of 87 high-quality candidates and confirmed lensing systems found in CFHTLS data. It includes a sample of 33 confirmed lenses from the <span class=\"small-caps\">RingFinder</span> (Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib33\">2014</a>), the 26 most promising candidates from <span class=\"small-caps\">ArcFinder</span> as implemented in SL2S ARCS (SARCS; More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>), and 28 candidates from <span class=\"small-caps\">SpaceWarps</span> (More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib56\">2016</a>), giving a total of 87 objects; the Einstein radii have a range of 1'' &lt; <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> &lt; 18''. In the <span class=\"small-caps\">ArcFinder</span> search, 12 candidates have giant arcs, which canonically have length-to-width ratio \u22658. CFHTLS has an effective search area of ~150 deg<sup>2</sup> and depths of <em>g</em> ~ 25.5 and ~26.5 mag in wide and deep fields, respectively.</p><p>We estimate the lens-finding selection function of DES by counting the sources from the high-quality CFHTLS sample that survive a cut on the DES magnitude limit, which is taken to be 24.5 mag in <em>g</em>band for both the SV and full wide-field data. The source magnitudes of the samples listed above were provided by the author (A. More 2016, personal communication, 2015 November 11), and the calculation of the source magnitudes is discussed in Section 5.3 of More et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib56\">2016</a>). After performing the magnitude cut, we linearly scaled the resulting counts by the ratio of the survey areas. From this comparison, we estimate that the full DES Wide-field survey will contain 2100 promising candidates. Scaling the area again for the DES SV area, we expect 105 promising candidates, which is nearly twice the amount found in the search discussed in this work.</p><p>A few factors likely contributed to the difference between the number of lens system candidates expected and discovered in DES SV data. The nontargeted search was performed by scanning large images, rather than cutouts around particular objects, as was done with the targeted search around clusters. In addition, some regions were only scanned once during the nontargeted search. Rigorous characterization of a strong lens candidate selection function requires the construction of a framework to measure each scanner's efficiency, completeness, and purity. Such a program was not implemented for the SV searches.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s4\" name=\"apjaa23f2s4\">4.\u00a0SPECTROSCOPIC FOLLOW-UP</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We obtained spectroscopic follow-up observations with the Gemini Multi-object Spectrograph (GMOS; Hook et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib36\">2004</a>) on the Gemini South Telescope, as part of the Gemini Large and Long Program GS-2014B-LP-5,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn11\">57</a></sup>\n and with the Inamori-Magellan Areal Camera and Spectrograph (IMACS) multi-object spectrograph (Dressler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib26\">2011</a>) on the Magellan/Baade Telescope.</p><p>Of the 53 high-ranking candidates found during the lens search, we selected the 24 candidates most suitable for spectroscopic follow-up. For the purposes of planning the follow-up, the 24 candidates were ranked (1\u20133) by their surface brightness, which is calculated using a 2''-diameter aperture in the <em>i</em>band:</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">R1: <em>i</em><sub>SB</sub> &lt; 23 mag arcsec<sup>\u22122</sup> (6 candidates);</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">R2: 23 &lt; <em>i</em><sub>SB</sub> &lt; 24 mag arcsec<sup>\u22122</sup> (14 candidates);</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">R3: <em>i</em><sub>SB</sub> &gt; 24 mag arcsec<sup>\u22122</sup> (4 candidates).</div></li></ul><p>These rankings are not related to the quality rankings of candidates in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s3\">3</a>. Details of the observing parameters and conditions for each of our confirmed lensing systems are shown in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t2\">2</a>. We acquired spectroscopic follow-up for 21 of the 24 candidate systems: 17 systems were observed at Gemini and 5 at Magellan. One of the systems, DES J0329\u20132820, was observed at both Gemini and Magellan: only B600 data were acquired at Gemini, which presented no spectral features, so we observed at longer wavelengths at Magellan.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa23f2t2\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 2.</b>\u00a0\nSpectroscopic Observation Log\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" 22</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Gemini-GMOS</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">R150</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1</td>\n<td rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1/1.63</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.64/0.67</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">DES J2336\u20135352</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">2014 Oct 21</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Gemini-GMOS</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">R150</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.73</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Note.</strong> Observation log for follow-up spectroscopic observations. DES ID is derived from the R.A. and decl. position of the lensing object at the center of the system. For the Gemini observations, seeing is taken from the Gemini Observation Logs, which keep environmental conditions in 2 hr increments: the seeing for each exposure set is interpolated for the time of observation between adjacent recordings of the environmental log. For the Magellan observation, seeing is measured from the FWHM of the PSFs of the acquisition stars in the science exposures.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t2_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/0004-637X/827/1/51\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t2_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We searched the Master Lens Database (L. Moustakas &amp; J. Brownstein 2016, in preparation)<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn12\">58</a></sup>\n and the Gemini Science Archive<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn13\">59</a></sup>\n to ascertain whether any of our candidates had been previously discovered or confirmed. The Master Lens Database (last updated 2015 November 17) contains 657 candidates and confirmed systems. Out of these systems, 44 fall within our search area but were not identified as candidates for our follow-up program. They were identified by various programs, such as SL2S, SPT, and searches in GEMS and <em>HST</em> fields. About half of these candidates have estimated Einstein radii <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> &lt; 2'', and the maximum radius is ~6''; at least 80% are galaxy-scale lenses. Of the 44 candidates, the lensing features are barely visible or better in DES SV data for about one-third of the systems, but they are only obvious for a few. The limited resolution of the DES images obscures lensing features, especially for smaller-radius systems. Additionally, many features are faint and are not obviously lensing features, but could be chance projection or on-edge spirals, for example.</p><p>The only candidate that is in our confirmed sample is SA39 (More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>), discovered by the SL2S in CFHTLS data. We have obtained the first spectroscopic confirmation that SA39 is a strong-lensing system. This is DES J0221\u20130646 in our sample, and we present its confirmation in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s5-1\">5.1</a>.</p><p>From the estimates of the selection function, we expect to find 105 promising candidates in DES, but we recovered 54. If all of the 44 missed candidates from the Master Lens Database are considered promising, then that would account for the discrepancy with our expectations described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s3\">3</a>. It is clear, however, that the image resolution is the principal factor in identification of systems with small Einstein radii.</p><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s4-1\" name=\"apjaa23f2s4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Gemini/GMOS</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We observed some of the lensing candidates at Gemini South using the multi-object mode on GMOS. The mask for each field was centered on the candidate lens of each system, and slits were placed on the candidate source images. In some cases, it was necessary to shift the field center to locate a suitable guide star. Accommodating as many source targets as possible sometimes required rotation of the slit mask (i.e., rotation of the position angle of each system). We placed slits on as many of the lensing features as possible, and the remaining ~50 slits were placed on galaxy targets for photometric-redshift calibration of DES data. The slits were 1'' in width and of varying length in order to accommodate both the object and an amount of sky sufficient to perform reliable background subtraction. In some cases, we tilted the slits to maximize the captured flux. We defined three sets of exposure times/grating combinations, which depend on the surface brightness classes defined above in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s4\">4</a>:</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">R1: 1 hr integration with R150/GG455 grating/filter;</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">R2: 1 hr integration with B600 grating and 3.7 hr integration with R150/GG455 grating/filter;</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">R3: 1 hr integration with B600 grating.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p><p>We use the R150 grating in conjunction with the GG455 filter in order to obtain spectra with wavelength coverage ~4500\u201310000 \u00c5. For the sources that we expect to be late-type emission-line galaxies, this would allow us, in most cases, to detect [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727 to <em>z</em> ~ 1.7, H<em>\u03b2</em> to <em>z</em> ~ 1.0, and Ly<em>\u03b1</em> in the range <em>z</em> ~ 2.7\u20137.2. We use the B600 grating to obtain spectral coverage of 3250\u20136250 \u00c5, which would allow us to detect sources with <em>z</em> &gt; 2.0 that emit Ly<em>\u03b1</em>.</p><p>A 1 hr observing sequence consisted of a pair of 900 s exposures, followed by a flat field taken with a quartz-halogen lamp and a calibration spectrum taken with a CuAr arc lamp. We then dithered to a different central wavelength to cover the gap between the CCDs and took a CuAr spectrum, followed by the flat-field exposure and then two more 900 s exposures. The 3.7 hr integration used the same sequence repeated 16 times, but with 840 s exposure times. Dividing the integration time into multiple exposures facilitates the removal of cosmic rays. The data were binned 2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 2, giving effective dispersions of 0.1 and 0.386 nm/pixel for the B600 and R150 gratings, respectively. The low-surface-brightness systems (i.e., rank R3) underwent only 1 hr of integration with the B600 grating. The goal of this work is to confirm and further analyze a set of strong-lensing systems: to conserve telescope time and maximize the number of lensing system confirmations, we elected to not pursue longer integrations for a given candidate system, even if a clear spectroscopic signal did not appear. In addition, the photo-<em>z</em> calibration targets require observations with consistent depth, and thus integration time, across the observation fields.</p><p>We used the Gemini IRAF package v2.16<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn14\">60</a></sup>\n to reduce all exposures. In each system, for each wavelength dither, we first process the flat field using the <tt>gsflat</tt> task (this includes subtraction of the bias). Each science exposure in a single dither is then reduced with <tt>gsreduce</tt> (using the previously processed flat fields), and then the two exposures are combined with <tt>gemcombine</tt>. Wavelength calibration and transformation are performed on each dither (using the <tt>gswavelength</tt> and <tt>gstransform</tt> tasks) before the pairs of dithers are coadded on a common wavelength scale to eliminate CCD chip gaps. We perform sky subtraction and one-dimensional (1D) spectral extraction using <tt>gsextract</tt>, which employs the <tt>apall</tt> task. Feature identification and redshift estimation are performed using the <tt>emsao</tt> task within the <tt>rvsao</tt> IRAF package (Kurtz &amp; Mink <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib45\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib45\">1998</a>). We modified some of the Gemini IRAF tasks to provide more flexibility in the data reduction. In particular, we modified <tt>gswavelength</tt> and <tt>gsextract</tt> to allow one to perform 1D and two-dimensional (2D) fits on the calibration data and sky subtraction on the science data, respectively, for individual extensions.</p><p>Of the 21 observed candidate systems, 17 were observed at Gemini: 12 systems were observed in Priority Visiting observer mode from 2014 October 19 to 24, and five systems were observed in queue mode. For nine systems, we obtained all the planned exposures, and for eight systems, we obtained a subset of the planned exposures. The only planned 1 hr integrations acquired with the R150 grating (rank R1) were executed during the Priority Visiting Observer mode. The eight systems for which we did not obtain all planned exposures are missing the planned 3.7 hr R150 observations: in queue mode, these observations had lower Gemini observing priority. All planned 1 hr B600 observations were completed.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s4-2\" name=\"apjaa23f2s4-2\">4.2.\u00a0Magellan/IMACS</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We also obtained spectra at Magellan with IMACS. The mask was again centered on the candidate lensing galaxy of each system. As with GMOS, we used 1''-width slits of varying length. We used the f/2 camera and the 200-<em>\u2113</em>/mm grism, because it has the best response in the redder wavelengths, and we used the WB5650-9200 filter to set the wavelength range to 5650-9200 \u00c5. We took three 1200 s exposures for each mask to facilitate cosmic-ray removal. Two of the systems observed also had a 1 hr integration using the B600 grating using Gemini's GMOS. A ranking system was not used to select which systems to prioritize for observation.</p><p>To process the data, we used the COSMOS data reduction package.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn15\">61</a></sup>\n This package performs wavelength calibration, followed by subtraction of bias, and division by flats, using the programs <tt>Sflats</tt> and <tt>biasflat</tt>. The sky is then subtracted using the program <tt>subsky</tt>. Finally, extraction of individual 1D or 2D spectral exposures is performed using <tt>extract</tt>, or <tt>extract-2dspec</tt>. Multiple sets of extracted spectra are then combined using <tt>sumspec</tt>. Feature identification and redshift estimation are performed using the <tt>emsao</tt> IRAF task.</p><p>Of the 21 observed candidate systems, 5 were observed on 2014 December 20\u201321 as part of a larger program to obtain spectroscopic redshifts of (1) supernova hosts and (2) galaxies for photometric redshift calibration of DES data. Note that one system, DES J0329\u20132820, was observed at both Magellan and Gemini.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s5\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5\">5.\u00a0CONFIRMED LENS SAMPLE</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Of the 24 systems for which we sought follow-up, there was sufficient time to observe 21 systems. Of those, we confirmed a total of six strong-lensing systems. There are 15 systems that were observed but were not confirmed as lenses: nine have no discernible continuum emission (nor spectral features), four have continuum emission but no discernible features, and two were ruled out as lenses.</p><p>A number of the systems that we failed to confirm exhibit clear morphological and photometric lensing features. However, they would require significantly longer integration times or are located in the redshift desert\u2014outside the range of the optical observations in our observing program. This highlights the challenge of obtaining spectra for the very faint objects found in deep optical surveys: a great deal of integration time, in tandem with IR spectrographs, may be required to confirm the large lens samples predicted in current and future large surveys. The candidates that were ruled out as lensing systems indeed exhibit suggestive morphological features, but the candidate lensed sources were determined to be foreground galaxies through spectroscopic analysis.</p><p>A multi-panel plot of the six confirmed systems is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>, and Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t1\">1</a> lists the positions and photometry of the candidate lens and source(s) of each lensing system observed. The sample comprises one group-scale lens and five cluster-scale lenses. The group-scale lens DES J0221\u20130646 is known from the SARCS sample by SL2S (More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>), but had not been spectroscopically confirmed by that group. It was found during the nontargeted phase of our visual scan and later identified as a low-richness (&lt;5) group in the redMAPPer catalog. Four lenses (DES J0250\u20130008, DES J0329\u20132820, DES J0446\u20135126, and DES J2336\u20135352) were also identified in the redMAPPer cluster-finder sample. DES J0330\u20135228 (SPT-CL J0330\u20135228/ACT-CL J0330\u20135227; Bleem et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib11\">2015</a>) is a known candidate lensing systems from the SPT and Atacama Cosmology Telescope (ACT; Kosowsky <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib41\">2006</a>; Fowler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib31\">2007</a>) SZ surveys. DES J2336\u20135352 (SCSO J233607\u2013535235; Menanteau et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib53\">2010a</a>) is already known from the Southern Cosmology Survey (SCS; Menanteau et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib52\">2009</a>). However, there were previously no spectroscopic observations of the lensing features in either of these systems. DES J0221\u20130646 and DES J0250\u20130008 were found in the slightly deeper DES supernova fields during the nontargeted searches.</p><p>In this section, we comment on each system, its spectral features of interest, and the derived redshifts. The reduced 1D spectra, alongside cutouts of the fields centered on the central lensing object showing the slit positions, targets, and Einstein radii, are shown for each system in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f2\">2</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f7\">7</a>. Detailed algorithms do not yield mass model constraints that are signficantly better than enclosed masses (derived from Einstein radii) for systems that have a limited number of sources with multiple, detailed images.</p><p>Therefore, for each system, we employ a strategy similar to that of SBAS (e.g., Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib25\">2009</a>) and estimate the enclosed mass <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> of the lensing system, assuming a singular isothermal sphere mass profile (Narayan &amp; Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib59\">1996</a>):</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa23f2eqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2eqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{M}_{\\mathrm{enc}}=\\displaystyle \\frac{{c}^{2}}{4G}{\\theta }_{{\\rm{E}}}^{2}\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{D}_{{\\rm{L}}}{D}_{{\\rm{S}}}}{{D}_{\\mathrm{LS}}}\\right),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>c</em> is the speed of light, <em>G</em> is Newton's gravitational constant, <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> is the Einstein radius, and <em>D</em> is the angular diameter distance. We estimate the Einstein radius (or radii if there is more than one source) by manually fitting a circle that passes through one or more spectroscopically confirmed features. The center is chosen to be the center of curvature of the candidate arc image(s). <em>D</em><sub>L</sub>, <em>D</em><sub>S</sub>, and <em>D</em><sub>LS</sub> are angular diameter distances to the lens, to the source, and between the lens and source, respectively: they depend only on the redshifts of these objects and on the cosmological parameters. The spectral features, photometric redshifts of lenses, spectroscopic redshifts of sources, Einstein radii, and enclosed masses are summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t3\">3</a>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa23f2t3\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 3.</b>\u00a0\nLensing Features\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2t3\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t3_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 3\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">System ID and</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Lens Redshift</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Spec. colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.8972 \u00b1 0.0004</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">9 \u00b1 2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">4 \u00b1 2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Note.</strong> Lensing features of confirmed systems and properties of associated redMAPPer clusters. In the left section, we show the DESDM object IDs for lenses (as in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t1\">1</a>), the source image IDs, and the emission lines identified in the source spectra. In the middle section, we show the photometric redshifts of lenses <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub>, spectroscopic redshifts <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub>, Einstein radii <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>, and derived enclosed masses <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub>. Ellipsis points indicate features that were not measured. For all of the systems, photometric redshifts (drawn from the the DESDM database) were obtained for the lensing galaxies. The uncertainties have been multiplied by 1.5 times the original estimate, according to the results of S\u00e1nchez et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib69\">2014</a>) for estimating uncertainties in DES photometric redshift measurement codes. The photometric redshifts of the sources within the DESDM database were not found to be reliable when compared with the spectroscopic redshift measurements made in this work: the source redshifts are too large for accurate photometric estimations. Redshifts for the sources are those measured from spectroscopic follow-up at Gemini South and Magellan; please see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s4\">4</a> for details of the spectroscopic follow-up. Only one lensing galaxy has a spectroscopic redshift: in DES J0330\u20135228, we used that measured by Werner et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>). For five of the six systems, morphology indicates a single Einstein radius and enclosed mass for a given system. For each of the systems DES J0221\u20130646 and DES J0446\u20135126, we measured a single Einstein radius that intersects two images with the same source redshift. DES J0250\u20130008 and DES J0330\u20135228 each have only one source image with redshifts. DES J0329\u20132820 has two source redshifts, but only one is chosen for the radius measurement. In DES J2336\u20135352, the lower-redshift source has, anomalously, a larger radius; arc B may be outside the caustic and thus only weakly magnified and not multiply imaged. Please see Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s5-1\">5.1</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s5-6\">5.6</a> for details of the analysis in each system. In the rightmost section, we show the properties of the associated redMAPPer clusters. The masses are measured with the methods of Saro et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib70\">2015</a>). The mass calibration method does not reach to low enough masses and richness to provide a complete probability distribution for the mass of this DES J0221\u20130646; we instead cite an upper limit. The redMAPPer redshifts are measured for the entire cluster with the colors of all the cluster members, which affords a much smaller uncertainty in the redMAPPer redshifts than that of the lensing galaxy for all systems.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t3_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/0004-637X/827/1/51\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/suppdata/apjaa23f2t3_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>All of the confirmed systems are associated with redMAPPer groups or clusters. The cluster masses were measured with the procedure of Saro et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib70\">2015</a>), which uses redMAPPer and SPT clusters to calibrate richness and mass. The spherical overdensity mass <em>M</em><sub>\u0394</sub> is that enclosed within a radial boundary demarcated by a density contrast \u0394 with respect to the critical density. We summarize the redshifts, richnesses, and masses of these clusters in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t3\">3</a>. The redMAPPer cluster redshifts are measured with the colors of all the cluster members, which affords a much smaller uncertainty in the cluster redshifts than that of the lensing galaxy for all systems. There are then likely to be some differences between the redshift of the single lensing galaxy and that of the cluster. However, except for the group-scale system, DES J0221\u20130646, the photometric redshift of the lensing galaxy and the redMAPPer-determined redshift agree within uncertainties\u2014as they should, since the lensing galaxy is within the cluster.</p><p>We estimate frequentist uncertainties for the enclosed mass, which include uncertainties propagated from redshifts (via the angular diameter distances) and Einstein radii. The photometric redshift uncertainties have been multiplied by a factor of 1.5, according to the prescription of S\u00e1nchez et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib69\">2014</a>) for photometric redshift measurements in DES. The spectroscopic redshift uncertainties are the result of a sum in quadrature of the wavelength calibration uncertainty and the redshift determination uncertainty from the IRAF function <tt>emsao</tt>.</p><p>The mass uncertainty is the sum in quadrature of the contributions from the Einstein radius and the three angular diameter distances. The uncertainty on each angular diameter distance scales with the redshift error, which varies from ~0.01% to ~5%, while uncertainty in the Einstein radius is at least ~15% among all systems. This disparity sets the Einstein radius as the dominant contributor to the uncertainty. In the manual fit, the uncertainty in the radius arises from variation of the arc's curvature over its extent, with the assumption that the correct center is chosen, as defined in Diehl et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib25\">2009</a>). These uncertainties are estimated to be up to a few arcseconds. The distances between the source and lens cause some variation in the relative contributions to uncertainties: smaller distances provide for a greater contribution from the angular diameter distance <em>D</em><sub>LS</sub>. This results in mass uncertainties in the range ~30%\u201380% (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2t3\">3</a>).</p><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-1\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-1\">5.1.\u00a0DES J0221\u20130646</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0221\u20130646 was originally discovered in CFHTLS (Object SA39; More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>) and encountered during our nontargeted search. It was also serendipitously found to be a group-scale lens in the redMAPPer catalog, with richness 4.64 \u00b1 1.15 and redshift 0.603 \u00b1 0.025. The central red galaxy of the lens has a DESDM photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.672 \u00b1 0.042. There are two prominent blue arcs, A1 and A2, shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(a), to the southeast and southwest of the lensing galaxy, respectively. These arcs are also shown in the top and middle panels on the right of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f2\">2</a>. In the follow-up GMOS spectroscopy of both arcs, we identify strong emission lines (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f2\">2</a>, left panel) at the same observed wavelength (4535 \u00c5) in both B600 spectra. Taking into account the absence of other spectral features in the R150 spectra, along with the photometric redshift of the lens galaxy, we assign the features to be Ly<em>\u03b1</em>, which gives redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 2.7251 \u00b1 0.0008 and 2.7241 \u00b1 0.0008 for arcs A1 and A2, respectively.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0DES J0221\u20130646. The 1D spectra for A1 and A2 are shown on the left. There are strong emission lines at the same observed wavelength 4535 \u00c5 in both spectra. Taking into account the absence of other spectral features in the R150 spectra, along with the photometric redshift of the lens galaxy, we assign the features to be Ly<em>\u03b1</em>, which gives redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 2.7251 \u00b1 0.0008 and 2.7241 \u00b1 0.0008 for A1 and A2, respectively. The orientation of the slits (red boxes) is shown in the upper right DES <em>g</em>-band image. In the color coadd image in the middle right panel, the two features of interest are labeled \"A1\" and \"A2,\" the Einstein radius is marked by a white arc, and the small scale bar shows the size of the image. The flat, zero-count portions of the spectra near <em>\u03bb</em> ~ 4200 and ~5300 \u00c5 show the chip gaps in the detectors: we did not take an offset observation to cover the chip gaps for this system.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We estimate an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 6'' \u00b1 1'', which passes through both arcs, giving an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (1 \u00b1 0.4) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</p><p>In the DES imaging, there appear two arcs that may be counterimages. An arc north of arc A2, and extremely close to the lensing galaxy, may be a counterimage to arc A1. These objects appear more clearly in Figure 3, panel \"39\" of More et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>). If we assume that arc A1 has a counterpart to the northeast, we can perform a manual measurement of the image-splitting distance: for lensing systems where the source lies nearly along the line of sight through the lens, the distance between an image and its counterimage is related to the Einstein radius, \u0394<em>\u03b8</em> ~ 2<em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>. For this system, \u0394<em>\u03b8</em> = 8'' \u00b1 2'', which gives <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> ~ 4'' \u00b1 2'', in agreement with the direct manual measurement of the Einstein radius.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0DES J0250\u20130008. We associate this feature with [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, which yields a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.2081 \u00b1 0.0004. These counterimages were not targeted: for a single multi-slit mask, it was not possible to obtain spectra for these objects simultaneously with that of the brighter arcs we had chosen (see top right panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f2\">2</a> for the configuration of the mask slits).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-2\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-2\">5.2.\u00a0DES J0250\u20130008</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0250\u20130008 is a newly discovered system identified in the redMAPPer cluster sample during the targeted search. The central red lensing galaxy has a photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.841 \u00b1 0.042, with two blue features labeled A and B in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(b) and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f3\">3</a> (top and middle right panels); the arcs are located east and north of the central cluster galaxy, respectively. We identify a strong emission line for A (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f3\">3</a>, left panel) in the GMOS R150 spectrum at an observed wavelength of 8229 \u00c5. We associate this feature with [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, which yields a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.2081 \u00b1 0.0004. We do not detect any continuum, nor any emission or absorption lines, from arc B.</p><p>While we do not detect features in the arc B spectrum, its morphology suggests that it is likely to be a lensed source image. Assuming that it is a feature of this system, we located the lensing mass center such that it aligns with the tangential arc B. As the circle of the Einstein radius passes through both images A and B, we use the spectroscopic redshift of arc A, and we estimate a single Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 7'' \u00b1 2'' for this system, which gives an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (4 \u00b1 2)  <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. The source of image A may lie outside the caustic and thus may not be strongly lensed, only weakly magnified. In such a case, this would provide a poor estimate of the enclosed mass.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-3\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-3\">5.3.\u00a0DES J0329\u20132820</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0329\u20132820 is also a newly discovered system identified in the redMAPPer galaxy cluster sample during the targeted search. The central red galaxy has a photometric redshift <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.655 \u00b1 0.033. There are two small blue arcs, A and B, to the west and north of the central red galaxy in the image, shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(c) and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f4\">4</a>, top and middle right panels. In the follow-up IMACS spectroscopy of the arcs, we identify emission lines at two different observed wavelengths of 6694 and 8563 \u00c5. The GMOS B600 data for this system provide no observed features at shorter wavelengths; therefore, we identify the emission line as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, from which we obtain redshifts <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.7963 \u00b1 0.0001 and 1.2976 \u00b1 0.0003 for images A and B, respectively (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f4\">4</a>, left panel).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0DES J0329\u20132820. Given the lack of observed features at shorter wavelengths, we identify the emission as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, from which we obtain redshifts <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.7963 \u00b1 0.0001 and 1.2976 \u00b1 0.0003 for A and B, respectively. We therefore elect to use only A (the circle for which also goes through B). We then estimate a single Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 7'' \u00b1 2'' for A, which gives an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (6 \u00b1 4) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-4\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-4\">5.4.\u00a0DES J0330\u20135228</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0330\u20135228 was discovered with <em>XMM-Newton</em>\u2014via detection of a second X-ray peak (Werner et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>)\u2014as a cluster behind Abell 3128. It has also been measured in both the SPT (SPT-CL J0330\u20135228; Ruel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib67\">2014</a>) and ACT SZ surveys (ACT-CL J0330\u20135227; Menanteau et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib54\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib54\">2010b</a>). The central red galaxy has a photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.463 \u00b1 0.046. There is a very prominent blue arc (A) in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(d) to the southwest of the central lensing galaxy. In the follow-up GMOS R150 spectroscopy of the blue arc, we identify a strong emission line at an observed wavelength of 9146 \u00c5, which we take to be [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, from which we obtain a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.4541 \u00b1 0.0004 (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f5\">5</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0DES J0330\u20135228. The 1D spectrum for A is shown on the left. There is a strong emission line at an observed wavelength of 9146 \u00c5, which we take to be [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727, from which we obtain a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.4541 \u00b1 0.0004.
