In the color coadd image in the middle right panel, the feature of interest is labeled \"A,\" the Einstein radius is marked by a white arc, and a small scale bar shows the size of the image. In the lower right is an identical color coadd image. All images are oriented north up, east left.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We placed the center to the northeast to coincide with the arc's curvature. We estimate an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 8'' \u00b1 2'' for this system. Instead of the DES photometric redshift, we used the spectroscopic redshift from Werner et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>), <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.43961 \u00b1 0.00014, to estimate an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (2 \u00b1 0.8) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. Werner et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib80\">2007</a>) also estimate an Einstein radius of ~6<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 and an upper limit on the mass of ~5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>12</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>; the authors noted that they only had a redshift measurement for the lens. Without a spectroscopic redshift for the source, the authors assumed a combined angular diameter distance of <em>D</em><sub>L</sub> <em>D</em><sub>S</sub>/<em>D</em><sub>LS</sub> = 1 Gpc, which corresponds to an overestimate of the source redshift and thus an underestimate of the mass (see Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2eqn1\">1</a>)).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-5\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-5\">5.5.\u00a0DES J0446\u20135126</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J0446\u20135126 is also a newly discovered system, identified in the redMAPPer cluster-finder sample. Two small blue arcs, B1 and B2, lie to the northwest and southwest, respectively, of the central red galaxy; this is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(e) and in the top and middle right panels of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f6\">6</a>. The candidate lensing galaxy has a photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.746 \u00b1 0.047, in agreement with the redMAPPer cluster photometric redshift, <em>z</em><sub>RM</sub> = 0.734 \u00b1 0.020.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0DES J0446\u20135126. The 1D spectra for B1 and B2 are shown on the left. The spectrum for A is not shown, but has an emission line consistent with an interpretation as a foreground object. There are prominent emission lines in both arcs at the same observed wavelength of 5117 \u00c5. We conclude that this is Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission, from which we obtain redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 3.2068 \u00b1 0.0010 for B and 3.2086 \u00b1 0.0011 for C. The object labeled A also has emission lines (not shown), which we interpret as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727 and [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>5007. This gives a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.1680 \u00b1 0.0009, making object A a foreground object. The orientation of the slits (red boxes) is shown in the upper right DES <em>g</em>-band image. In the color coadd image in the middle right panel the three features of interest are labeled \"A,\" \"B1,\" and \"B2,\" the Einstein radius is marked by a white arc, and the small scale bar shows the size of the image. In the lower right is an identical color coadd image. All images are oriented north up, east left.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The GMOS B600 spectroscopy reveals prominent emission lines (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f6\">6</a>, left panel) in both arcs at the same observed wavelength of 5117 \u00c5. Given the appreciable strength of these emission lines in the B600 spectra, we expected to see other strong lines, such as H<em>\u03b2</em> in the R150 spectrum. However, additional features did not appear, and we therefore concluded that this emission line is Ly<em>\u03b1</em>, from which we obtain spectroscopic redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 3.2068 \u00b1 0.0010 and 3.2086 \u00b1 0.0011 for B1 and B2, respectively. The object labeled A in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(e) also has emission lines (not shown), which we interpret as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727 and [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>5007. This gives a redshift of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 0.1680 \u00b1 0.0009, making object A a foreground object.</p><p>We estimate an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 7'' \u00b1 2'' for this system, which gives an enclosed mass of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (2 \u00b1 0.9) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa23f2s5-6\" name=\"apjaa23f2s5-6\">5.6.\u00a0DES J2336\u20135352</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>DES J2336\u20135352 is a galaxy cluster originally found in the SCS, and it has a cluster photometric redshift of <em>z</em><sub>c</sub> = 0.54 (SCSO J233607\u2013535235; Menanteau et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib53\">2010a</a>). It is also found in the redMAPPer cluster-finder sample, which gives a photometric redshift <em>z</em><sub>RM</sub> = 0.522 \u00b1 0.011, in agreement with the DES photometric redshift, <em>z</em><sub>lens</sub> = 0.530 \u00b1 0.075.</p><p>There are two small red arcs, A and B, to the west and southeast of the central red galaxy, respectively. These are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f1\">1</a>(f) and in the top and middle right panels of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f7\">7</a>. The GMOS R150 spectra of these two arcs show prominent emission lines at two different observed wavelengths\u20148023 \u00c5 for A and 7070 \u00c5 for B\u2014which we identify as [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727. These yield source redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.1528 \u00b1 0.0006 and 0.8972 \u00b1 0.0004 for A and B, respectively (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa23f2f7\">7</a>, left panel).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa23f2f7\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa23f2f7\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f7_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 7.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 7.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/827/1/51/Full/apjaa23f2f7_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 7.</strong>\u00a0DES J2336\u20135352. The 1D spectra for A and B are shown on the left. The prominent emission lines at two different observed wavelengths of 8023 \u00c5 for A and 7070 \u00c5 for B are due to [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727. These lines yield source spectroscopic redshifts of <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub> = 1.1528 \u00b1 0.0006 and 0.8972 \u00b1 0.0004 for A and B, respectively. The orientation of the slits (red boxes) is shown in the upper right DES <em>g</em>-band image. In the color coadd image in the middle right panel the two features of interest are labeled \"A\" and \"B.\" The Einstein radii are marked by white and red arcs for slits A and B, respectively. The small scale bar shows the size of the image. In the lower right is an identical color coadd image. All images are oriented north up, east left.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa23f2f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa23f2f7\" href=\"/0004-637X/827/1/51/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa23f2f7\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We estimate an Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 5'' \u00b1 2'' for A and <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> = 9'' \u00b1 2'' for B, which give enclosed masses of <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (9 \u00b1 7) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>12</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> and <em>M</em><sub>enc</sub> = (4 \u00b1 2) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup><em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, respectively.</p><p>We note that normally one would expect the lower-redshift source to have a smaller <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub> than the higher-redshift one, but that does not seem to be the case here. Arc B may be outside the caustic, and thus only weakly magnified and not multiply imaged; it is also not clearly tangentially aligned with the candidate lens, which makes it less likely to be a lensed source.</p><p>There also exists a faint image to the west of arc B, almost directly south of the central red lensing galaxy, that may be another source or counterimage. To provide sufficient sky coverage in the slit for arc B, this image could not also be observed in a single mask.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s6\" name=\"apjaa23f2s6\">6.\u00a0DISCUSSION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Many of our unconfirmed systems exhibit clear lensing features but have no detectable continuum or spectral features, with integration times of about 1 hr on an 8 m class telescope.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn16\">62</a></sup>\n The need for long integration time highlights the challenge of obtaining spectra for faint objects found by deep optical surveys, like DES: the challenge will only be greater for the Large Synoptic Survey Telescope<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn17\">63</a></sup>\n (LSST; LSST Science Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib48\">2009</a>). One faces the prospect of long integration times, which limits the number of systems that can be followed up with a typical program. For systems that exhibit a clear lensing morphology and that have detectable continuum emission but no visible features, IR data may be necessary to confirm them.</p><p>A number of large telescopes housing UV to infrared spectroscopic instruments are planned for the coming 10\u201320 yr, which will help to alleviate this pressure. The Thirty Meter Telescope<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn18\">64</a></sup>\n will require a mere hour of integration on <em>i</em> ~ 25 for low-noise spectroscopic measurements\u2014similarly for the Giant Magellan Telescope.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn19\">65</a></sup>\n However, with their expected high subscription rate, it remains to be seen whether there will be enough time to follow up the systems predicted for projects like LSST.</p><p>While Gemini's GMOS instrument covers optical wavelengths in multi-slit or long-slit mode, the Very Large Telescope's X-shooter<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn20\">66</a></sup>\n provides long-slit capability for strong-lens follow-up simultaneously in optical and near-IR wavelengths. X-shooter is the first second-generation instrument on the 8 m VLT: it has an echelle spectrograph that (1) covers a spectral range 300\u20132500 nm at intermediate resolution (<em>R</em> ~ 4,000\u201317,000) and (2) operates in long-slit or IFU mode (Vernet et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib77\">2011</a>). In addition, new instruments are being proposed for the Gemini Observatories via the Gemini Instrument Feasibility study.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2fn21\">67</a></sup>\n For example, the Gemini Multi-Obect eXtra-wide-band Spectrograph (GMOX; Robberto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib66\">2015</a>) has a three-arm spectrograph covering the entire optical and near-IR spectrum that is available from the ground. Another example, OCTOCAM, is a multi-channel imager and long-slit spectrograph, also covering the optical and near-IR wavelengths (de Ugarte Postigo <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib22\">2015</a>). Both GMOX and OCTOCAM add integral field capability, which are ideal for extended lenses. If integration times are sufficiently low for these new instruments, the Gemini observatories may provide a competitive resource for follow-up of lensing systems in future surveys.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa23f2s7\" name=\"apjaa23f2s7\">7.\u00a0SUMMARY</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>In this paper we presented the first confirmed group- and cluster-scale strong-lensing systems found in DES data. These systems were discovered through visual identification using both targeted and nontargeted searches. We confirmed these systems with a combination of spectroscopy from GMOS on the Gemini South telescope and IMACS on the Magellan/Baade telescope.</p><p>Two of these systems are of particular interest because they have source galaxies at redshifts <em>z</em> = 2.7251 and <em>z</em> = 3.22086. Due to the magnification provided by lensing, these sources are typically among the brightest galaxies in their redshift ranges and can provide an important window into the star formation history and galaxy formation at these cosmic epochs.</p><p>The candidate lensing systems discovered in DES SV data span a range of arc sizes, much like the currently known sample of candidates from searches during the past decade. Due to the seeing limitations of DES (median expected seeing ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />9), systems with smaller arc separations were more difficult to identify, especially during the nontargeted search. Thus, our visual inspection methods likely yielded higher completeness in the targeted search of redMAPPer and SPT clusters.</p><p>As we examine more of the DES wide-field area, we expect to find many more systems. Through comparison with the CFHTLS data, we expect there to be ~2100 lenses in the full survey, which is similar to the number found in the preceding decade of survey searches in optical and IR wavelengths listed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa23f2s1\">1</a>. Independently, Collett (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib19\">2015</a>) predicts 800 galaxy-scale lenses.</p><p>The Year 1 DES data cover an area of ~1800 deg<sup>2</sup>, and we have already conducted targeted searches, identifying a number of promising systems for follow-up. We have also expanded the search techniques to use arc-finding (e.g., More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib57\">2012</a>) and ring-finding algorithms (e.g., Gavazzi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib33\">2014</a>), as well as catalog-based photometry searches similar to those employed in the SBAS (e.g., Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib25\">2009</a>). We continue the dedicated program, STRIDES (Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib76\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib76\">2015</a>), to search for lensed quasars. With these tools, our searches through Year 1 data will be more effective than in SV for finding galaxy-scale lenses. The high yields of DES and future surveys will likely pose a challenge for spectroscopic follow-up and thus a challenge to maximize strong lensing for use in constraining cosmology.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We are grateful for the extraordinary contributions of our CTIO colleagues and the DES Camera, Commissioning, and Science Verification teams for achieving excellent instrument and telescope conditions that have made this work possible. The success of this project also relies critically on the expertise and dedication of the DES Data Management organization.</p><p>Funding for the DES Projects has been provided by the U.S. Department of Energy, the U.S. National Science Foundation, the Ministry of Science and Education of Spain, the Science and Technology Facilities Council of the United Kingdom, the Higher Education Funding Council for England, the National Center for Supercomputing Applications at the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, the Kavli Institute of Cosmological Physics at the University of Chicago, the Center for Cosmology and Astro-Particle Physics at the Ohio State University, the Mitchell Institute for Fundamental Physics and Astronomy at Texas A&amp;M University, Financiadora de Estudos e Projetos, Funda\u00e7\u00e3o Carlos Chagas Filho de Amparo \u00e0 Pesquisa do Estado do Rio de Janeiro, Conselho Nacional de Desenvolvimento Cientfico e Tecnol\u00f3gico and the Minist\u00e9rio da Ci\u00eancia e Tecnologia, the Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft, and the Collaborating Institutions in the Dark Energy Survey. The DES data management system is supported by the National Science Foundation under Grant Number AST-1138766. The DES participants from Spanish institutions are partially supported by MINECO under grants AYA2012-39559, ESP2013-48274, FPA2013-47986, and Centro de Excelencia Severo Ochoa SEV-2012-0234, some of which include ERDF funds from the European Union.</p><p>The Collaborating Institutions are Argonne National Laboratory, the University of California at Santa Cruz, the University of Cambridge, Centro de Investigaciones Energ\u00e9ticas, Medioambientales y Tecnol\u00f3gicas-Madrid, the University of Chicago, University College London, the DES-Brazil Consortium, the Eidgenoessische Technische Hochschule (ETH) Zurich, Fermi National Accelerator Laboratory, the University of Edinburgh, the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, the Institut de Ci\u00e8ncies de l'Espai (IEEC/CSIC), the Institut de F\u00edsica d'Altes Energies, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, the Ludwig-Maximilians Universit\u00e4t and the associated Excellence Cluster Universe, the University of Michigan, the National Optical Astronomy Observatory, the University of Nottingham, the Ohio State University, the University of Pennsylvania, the University of Portsmouth, SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory, Stanford University, the University of Sussex, and Texas A&amp;M University.</p><p>We are also grateful to Janani Sivakumar, student at the Illinois Mathematics and Science Academy, as well as Caroline Odden and her students, Kathryn Chapman, George Avecillas, and Matthew Simon, at the Phillips Academy, for their contributions to the scanning effort. We also thank Matthew Becker and Risa Wechsler for helpful discussions on the topic of galaxy cluster richnesses and masses.</p><p>This work is based in part on observations obtained at the Gemini Observatory, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy, Inc., under a cooperative agreement with the NSF on behalf of the Gemini partnership: the National Science Foundation (United States), the National Research Council (Canada), CONICYT (Chile), the Australian Research Council (Australia), Minist\u00e9rio da Ci\u00eancia, Tecnologia e Inova\u00e7\u00e3o (Brazil), and Ministerio de Ciencia, Tecnolog\u00eda e Innovaci\u00f3n Productiva (Argentina). The data were acquired through the Gemini Science Archive and processed using the Gemini IRAF package v2.16.</p><p>Thanks to the entire Las Campanas Observatory staff for their help in the acquisition of the Magellan/IMACS data (IMACS; Bigelow &amp; Dressler <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa23f2bib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa23f2bib8\">2003</a>). This paper includes data gathered with the 6.5 m Magellan Telescopes located at Las Campanas Observatory, Chile.</p><p>C.F. acknowledges funding from CAPES (proc. 12203-1). Through visual inspection of data from the Science Verification season, we identified 53 candidate systems. We then obtained spectroscopic follow-up of 21 candidates using the Gemini Multi-object Spectrograph at the Gemini South telescope and the Inamori-Magellan Areal Camera and Spectrograph at the Magellan/Baade telescope. With this follow-up, we confirmed six candidates as gravitational lenses: three of the systems are newly discovered, and the remaining three were previously known. Of the 21 observed candidates, the remaining 15 either were not detected in spectroscopic observations, were observed and did not exhibit continuum emission (or spectral features), or were ruled out as lensing systems. The confirmed sample consists of one group-scale and five galaxy-cluster-scale lenses.
